y ∈ {0,1}  (binary classification)  ^zu4VEv5Z

x ^dArAnOVa

The case above, with a really simple dataset, if you threshold with 0.5, LR can work, but just with a new example you end up with a really different decision boundary.
So LR is not a good algorithm to classification. ^hpQkFMCs

0 ^qoyziAvB

Logistic regression ^M25Q2Xu8

By far, the most commonly used classification algorithm is ^siMkUtft

We want the h (x) ∈ [0,1], to fix this let's change the form of the hypotheses: ^W9ka6385

"sigmoid" or "logistic" function ^btNGdXQ2

Lets assume: ^Xozk5aNC

the chance of a tumor being malignant (y=1), given the size of the tumor (x), parameterized by θ, is equal to your hypotheses ^RipxdFz2

and since it can only be 1 or 0, by logic, the chance of y = 0, its 1 - hypothesis  ^TClNUSPo

Using maximum likelihood estimation again: ^3kO7tNza

Then, again, we want to find the value of θ that maximizes the likelihood of the parameters. ^DLZsBQoV

Chose θ to maximize l(θ);
    for this we will use: ^eJaBarKm

Batch gradient ascent ^sVq2UXsM

gradient ascent climb up the hill, while gradient descent (regression) climb down ^iV1FcO4i

with l(θ) there are no local optimal ^c5lTJYB8

Newton's method ^nsGNYq0k

Gradient ascent takes a lot of iterations to converge.
There is another algorithm that allows you to take much bigger jumps. ^iwp3oCQi

Have f.
Want to find θ 
    s.t. f(θ) = 0.
[Want to maximize l(θ)
    i.e. Want l'(θ) = 0.] ^jnZI7VhB

One iteration of newton's method is ^cKGLidcb

Let f(θ) = l'(θ) ^OnNqRLfY

Newton's method enjoys a property called 
"Quadratic convergence". ^bcFhSIFD

Newton's method is fast but can be expensive in very high-dimensional problems.
As a rule of thumb: if the number of parameters is not too big (10, 50) use Newton's method, but if you have a lot of parameters (10.000, 50.000), use gradient descent. ^ihCQ0waC
